<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome-wide association mapping of date palm fruit traits"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2019 10:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-10-15"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Date palms (Phoenix dactylifera) are an important fruit crop of arid regions of the Middle East and North Africa. Despite its importance, few genomic resources exist for date palms, hampering evolutionary genomic studies of this perennial species. Here we report an improved long-read genome assembly for P. dactylifera that is 772.3&#8201;Mb in length, with contig N50 of 897.2 Kb, and use this to perform genome-wide association studies (GWAS) of the sex determining region and 21 fruit traits. We find a fruit color GWAS at the R2R3-MYB transcription factor VIRESCENS gene and identify functional alleles that include a retrotransposon insertion and start codon mutation. We also find a GWAS peak for sugar composition spanning deletion polymorphisms in multiple linked invertase genes. MYB transcription factors and invertase are implicated in fruit color and sugar composition in other crops, demonstrating the importance of parallel evolution in the evolutionary diversification of domesticated species. Date palm is an important fruit crop in the Middle East and North Africa. Here, the authors report an improved genome assembly of this species and perform GWAS mapping of sex determining region and 21 fruit traits using high density SNP data generated from re-sequencing of the mapping population."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Date palms (<i>Phoenix dactylifera</i>) are an important fruit crop of arid regions of the Middle East and North Africa. Despite its importance, few genomic resources exist for date palms, hampering evolutionary genomic studies of this perennial species. Here we report an improved long-read genome assembly for <i>P. dactylifera</i> that is 772.3âMb in length, with contig N50 of 897.2 Kb, and use this to perform genome-wide association studies (GWAS) of the sex determining region and 21 fruit traits. We find a fruit color GWAS at the R2R3-MYB transcription factor <i>VIRESCENS</i> gene and identify functional alleles that include a retrotransposon insertion and start codon mutation. We also find a GWAS peak for sugar composition spanning deletion polymorphisms in multiple linked invertase genes. MYB transcription factors and invertase are implicated in fruit color and sugar composition in other crops, demonstrating the importance of parallel evolution in the evolutionary diversification of domesticated species.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Domesticated crop species originated over the last 12,000 years through a co-evolutionary process as wild plant species are exposed to new selective environments associated with human cultivation and use<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Meyer, R. S. &amp; Purugganan, M. D. Evolution of crop species: genetics of domestication and diversification. Nat. Rev. Genet. 14, 840â852 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d10202e844">1</a></sup>. There are ~1000 to ~2500 semi- and fully domesticated plant species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Meyer, R. S. &amp; Purugganan, M. D. Evolution of crop species: genetics of domestication and diversification. Nat. Rev. Genet. 14, 840â852 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d10202e848">1</a></sup>, and these species are largely responsible for providing food and fiber to human agricultural societies. There is great interest in dissecting the genetic and evolutionary mechanisms underlying domestication and diversification of crop species, both to illuminate general evolutionary principles as well as provide insights into the genetic basis of traits for crop improvement. Most genetic and evolutionary studies, however, have focused on annual crop species, primarily cereal crops. In contrast, much less is known about the evolutionary genetics of perennial crops such as fruit tree species.</p><p>The date palm (<i>Phoenix dactylifera</i> L.) is an iconic species and major crop of the Middle East and North Africa known for its sweet edible fruits. It is traditionally cultivated in oasis agrosystems in hot arid habitats that extend from Morocco to Egypt in North Africa, the Arabian Peninsula, Iraq, and Iran in the Middle East, and Pakistan and India in South Asia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Barrow, S. A monograph of Phoenix L. (Palmae: Coryphoideae). Kew Bull. 53, 513â575 (1998)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d10202e858">2</a></sup>. Date palms are a dioecious, obligate outcrossing, and highly heterozygous monocot species that are typically clonally propagated in agricultural contexts.</p><p>Date palms are one of the oldest domesticated perennial crops, with evidence of exploitation dating to ~7100 years before present (yBP) from Dalma Island, United Arab Emirates and As-Sabiyah, Kuwait<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Tengberg, M. Beginnings and early history of date palm garden cultivation in the Middle East. J. Arid Environ. 86, 139â147 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d10202e865">3</a></sup>. Date palm cultivation is evident from theÂ early Bronze Age (late 4th/early 3rd millennia BCE) in Mesopotamia and the Arabian peninsula, while it appears later in North Africa (end of 2nd millennium BCE)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Tengberg, M. Beginnings and early history of date palm garden cultivation in the Middle East. J. Arid Environ. 86, 139â147 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d10202e869">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Flowers, J. M. et al. Cross-species hybridization and the origin of North African date palms. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 1â8. &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1817453116&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10202e872">4</a></sup>. By the third millennium BCE, cuneiform texts in Sumerian, and later in Akkadian discuss date palm gardens in what is today Southern Iraq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Tengberg, M. Beginnings and early history of date palm garden cultivation in the Middle East. J. Arid Environ. 86, 139â147 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d10202e876">3</a></sup>.</p><p>The date palm holds an important place in the cultures of the Middle East and North Africa, appearing in major religious texts and iconography of the region. Date fruits were routine offerings in religious ceremonies in the 3rd millennium BCE and consumed in royal palaces together with figs, apples, and grapes. The Babylonian Code of Hammurabi (ca. 1754 BCE), one of the oldest legal texts in the world, prescribes regulations regarding date palm orchards<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Chao, C. T. &amp; Krueger, R. R. The date palm (Phoenix dactylifera l.): overview of biology, uses and cultivation. HortScience 42, 1077â1082 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d10202e883">5</a></sup>. In ancient Egypt, date palms were associated with the sun, and Rameses III (ca. 1186â1155 BCE) decreed that date gardens be planted in Heliopolis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wilkinson, A. Symbolism and design in ancient Egyptian gardens. Gard. Hist. 22, 1â17 (1994)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d10202e887">6</a></sup>. Theophrastus (ca. 350 BCE) discusses date palms in <i>Historia Plantarum</i>, and date palms were prominent in Phoenician and Carthaginian coinage ~2300 years ago.</p><p>After domestication, date palms diversified across the species range and today more than 3000 recognized varieties exhibit substantial variation in fruit-related traits such as color, size, moisture, and sugar content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Popenoe, W. Date growing in the Old and New Worlds. (West India Gardens, 1913)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d10202e898">7</a></sup>. Early evidence of the diversification of fruit traits comes from archeological date stones which changed in size and shape consistent with selection for larger fruits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Terral, J. F. et al. Insights into the historical biogeography of the date palm (Phoenix dactylifera L.) using geometric morphometry of modern and ancient seeds. J. Biogeogr. 39, 929â941 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d10202e902">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Gros-Balthazard, M. et al. The discovery of wild date palms in oman reveals a complex domestication history involving centers in the Middle East and Africa. Curr. Biol. 27, 2211â2218 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d10202e905">9</a></sup>. Genomic data suggests that interspecific hybridization contributed to the diversification of cultivated date palms based on evidence of introgressive hybridization in North Africa between Middle Eastern cultivated date palms and the wild Cretan palm <i>P. theophrasti</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Flowers, J. M. et al. Cross-species hybridization and the origin of North African date palms. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 1â8. &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1817453116&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10202e911">4</a></sup>.</p><p>Date palms continue to be important to the economy and food security of the Middle East/North Africa. The importance of this crop has raised significant interest in the genetic basis of trait variation in this species both to aid future breeding efforts and understand the process of perennial crop domestication. However, long generation times (~4â6 years to first flowering), multiple years to generate offshoots for propagation, and 10â15 years to reach maximum yield all complicate multi-generation experiments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Krueger, R. R. Date palm germplasm: overview and utilization in the USA. In Proc. International Conference on Date Palms (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d10202e918">10</a></sup>. This has hindered development of quantitative trait loci (QTL) mapping populations and efforts at breeding date palms for crop improvement. In contrast to other fruit crops, there are presently few populations suitable for QTL mapping in date palms, and breeding efforts are scarce or have been terminated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Krueger, R. R. Date palm germplasm: overview and utilization in the USA. In Proc. International Conference on Date Palms (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d10202e922">10</a></sup>.</p><p>The difficulty of applying traditional genetic approaches has spurred the development of genomic resources to address problems in date palm cultivation and accelerate the discovery of important trait genes. Genome-wide association studies (GWAS) of tree crops provide an attractive alternative to QTL mapping approaches to identify loci controlling important traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Teh, C. K. et al. Genome-wide association study identifies three key loci for high mesocarp oil content in perennial crop oil palm. Sci. Rep. &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1038/srep19075&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d10202e929">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Larsen, B. et al. Genome-wide association studies in apple reveal loci for aroma volatiles, sugar composition, and harvest date. Plant Genome 12, 18104 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d10202e932">12</a></sup>. In date palms, GWAS not only circumvent problems associated with long generation times, but high levels of nucleotide diversity and a relatively rapid decay of linkage disequilibrium (LD) [~20â30âkb]<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Flowers, J. M. et al. Cross-species hybridization and the origin of North African date palms. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 1â8. &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1817453116&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10202e936">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Hazzouri, K. M. et al. Whole genome re-sequencing of date palms yields insights into diversification of a fruit tree crop. Nat. Commun. 6, 8824 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d10202e939">13</a></sup> should enable high resolution mapping. GWAS mapping is facilitated by the availability of good quality genome assemblies, and in date palm two draft genome assemblies of ~690âMb of the Khalas variety have been released<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Al-Dous, E. K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 521â527 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d10202e943">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Al-Mssallem, I. S. et al. Genome sequence of the date palm Phoenix dactylifera L. Nat. Commun. 4, 2274 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d10202e946">15</a></sup>. However, published assemblies are highly fragmented with N50 of ~30 and 330âkb and ~57,000 and 82,000 sequence fragments, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Al-Dous, E. K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 521â527 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d10202e950">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Al-Mssallem, I. S. et al. Genome sequence of the date palm Phoenix dactylifera L. Nat. Commun. 4, 2274 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d10202e953">15</a></sup>. Nevertheless, these genomes have enabled new discoveries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Gros-balthazard, M., Hazzouri, K. M. &amp; Flowers, J. M. Genomic insights into date palm origins. Genes 9, 1â14 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d10202e957">16</a></sup>, including identification and characterization of theÂ sex determination locus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Al-Dous, E. K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 521â527 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d10202e962">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Torres, M. F. et al. Genus-wide sequencing supports a two-locus model for sex-determination in Phoenix. Nat. Commun. 9, 3969 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d10202e965">17</a></sup>, discovery of pathways active during fruit maturation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Al-Mssallem, I. S. et al. Genome sequence of the date palm Phoenix dactylifera L. Nat. Commun. 4, 2274 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d10202e969">15</a></sup>, and genome-wide studies of diversity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Flowers, J. M. et al. Cross-species hybridization and the origin of North African date palms. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 1â8. &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1817453116&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10202e973">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Gros-Balthazard, M. et al. The discovery of wild date palms in oman reveals a complex domestication history involving centers in the Middle East and Africa. Curr. Biol. 27, 2211â2218 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d10202e976">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Hazzouri, K. M. et al. Whole genome re-sequencing of date palms yields insights into diversification of a fruit tree crop. Nat. Commun. 6, 8824 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d10202e979">13</a></sup>.</p><p>To facilitate GWAS in date palms, we generate an improved genome assembly from single molecule real time (SMRT) sequencing of a male date palm and conduct whole genome resequencing of a diverse set of male and female varieties located in two farms in the United Arab Emirates. The dense single nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) genotyping data from re-sequencing in conjunction with this assembly allow us to perform GWAS on key traits in this fruit crop that is of vital importance to the culture and economy of the Middle East and North Africa.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Long-read sequence assembly of a date palm genome</h3><p>To improve on published date palm genome assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Al-Dous, E. K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 521â527 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d10202e998">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Al-Mssallem, I. S. et al. Genome sequence of the date palm Phoenix dactylifera L. Nat. Commun. 4, 2274 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1001">15</a></sup>, we use Pacific Biosciences (PacBio) sequencing to sequence a male date palm from a fourth-generation backcross (BC4) with a female of the Barhee cultivar (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig1">1a</a>, TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Tab1">1</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Krueger, R. R. Date palm germplasm: overview and utilization in the USA. In Proc. International Conference on Date Palms (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1011">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Nixon, R. W. &amp; Furr, J. R. Problems and Progress In Date Breeding. (Coachella, 1965)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1014">18</a></sup>. We generated ~6.4 million long reads totaling 72âGb of data (mean subread lengthâ=â11.2âkb, read length N50â=â18.5âkb) and supplement the PacBio sequences with ~110 million reads from 2âÃâ100âbp paired-end Illumina short insert libraries totaling 10.3âGb of raw data. This results in a ~92.9Ã and ~13.8Ã fold-coverage of long and short reads, respectively (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12604-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12604_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12604-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12604_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="957" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>BC4 male date palm genome assembly. <b>a</b> Genome assembly work flow. <b>b</b> Total (anchoredâ+âunplaced) numbers of contigs. <b>c</b> Total (anchoredâ+âunplaced) contig lengths. <b>d</b> Length distribution of haplotigs and primary contigs generated by FALCON-Unzip. <b>e</b> Gene counts in the original (All) gene set and high confidence (Final) set. Pie diagrams indicate the percentage of genes with Pfam domains in each set. <b>f</b> Summary of BUSCO analysis of assembly completeness. <b>g</b> Circos diagram of 18 linkage groups showing gene density (inner) and linkage group ideograms (outer). Linkage groups are numbered in clockwise order beginning with LG1. Gene densities were calculated in sliding windows of 200âkb with step size of 50âkb. The source data underlying Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM10">1bâg</a> are provided as a Source Data file</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of the BC4 male date palm assembly and the previously published cultivar Khalas assemblies</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Using the FALCON-Unzip assembler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Chin, C.-S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1233">19</a></sup>, we created a genome assembly with 2706 primary contigs and 9753 haplotigs (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig1">1b, c</a>, Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">2</a>). The polished primary assembly spans 772.3âMb and has high contiguity with contig N50 of 897.2âkb, while haplotigs consist of 547.4âMb with N50 of 70.9âkb (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Tab1">1</a>, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig1">1d</a>, Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">2</a>). From one-step flow cytometry, we estimated total genome size of <i>P. dactylifera</i> to be 870â899âMb, indicating our draft assembly was 86â89% of the estimated size. We find our draft assembly is highly complete, with 98.05% of Illumina short reads, 94.2% of 7097 expressed sequence tags (ESTs) and &gt;98% of RNA-Seq reads aligning to our BC4 male assembly (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">3</a>). A BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: Assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1265">20</a></sup> analysis of genome completeness indicates that &gt;92.4% (1331 of 1440) of single copy ortholog groups in plants were recovered in the primary assembly, and only 5.1% were missing (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig1">1e, f</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">4</a>). Our assembly shows an increase in assembly size of ~18%, a ~2.7-fold increase in N50, and ~20-fold fewer assembly fragments compared to previous draft genome assemblies (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Tab1">1</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Al-Dous, E. K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 521â527 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1279">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Al-Mssallem, I. S. et al. Genome sequence of the date palm Phoenix dactylifera L. Nat. Commun. 4, 2274 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1282">15</a></sup>.</p><p>We use a low-resolution genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Mathew, L. S. et al. A first genetic map of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera) reveals long-range genome structure conservation in the palms. BMC Genomics 15, 285 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1289">21</a></sup> to assign primary contigs to 18 linkage groups (LGs) in the date palm genome. Using ALLMAPS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Tang, H. et al. ALLMAPS: robust scaffold ordering based on multiple maps. Genome Biol. 16, 3 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1293">22</a></sup>, we anchored 49.9% (385.6 of 772.3âMb) and orient 29.2% (225.8âMb) of primary contig sequence to the 18 LGs (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">1</a>). Linkage group 1 is the longest with 40.8âMb of anchored sequence, and LG 18 the shortest with 9.8âMb (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig1">1g</a>, Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">6</a>). The ALLMAPS anchoring approach produces expected syntenic relationships between date palm LGs and oil palm pseudomolecules, including the observation that oil palm chromosome 2 is comprised of a probable fusion of date palm chromosomes 1 and 10<sup>21</sup> (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><p>We annotated the BC4 male assembly by generating RNA-Seq reads from leaf, fruit, root, pollen, and flower and predict genes with these data, publicly available ESTs, and UniProt proteins as input to MAKER2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Holt, C. &amp; Yandell, M. MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinforma. 12, 491 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1318">23</a></sup> (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">7</a>). We predict 36,162 non-transposable elements (TE) protein-coding genes and 51,395 gene models (including isoforms), but restricted further analysis to a high-confidence set (i.e., only genes containing a Pfam domain) consisting of 43,815 isoforms encoded by 28,595 genes (Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">1</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">8</a>, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig1">1e</a>). The anchored assembly fraction was enriched in genes, with 66% of loci and 70% of isoforms located on the 18 LGs. Median gene size in the high-confidence set was ~4.2âkb, and median size of exons, CDS exons and introns were 159, 137, and 328âbps, respectively. Overall quality of the annotation was high, with 93.7% of high-confidence gene set models having an annotation edit distance (AED) score<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Holt, C. &amp; Yandell, M. MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinforma. 12, 491 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1335">23</a></sup> of 0.5 or lower (79.9% for the whole set, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">8</a>). We annotated theÂ repeat fraction of the genome and found more than half of the BC4 male assembly consists of repeats and TEs, with long-terminal repeat retrotransposons (LTR-RTs) occupying most of the TE space (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">9</a>), as reported for date palm and other palm species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Al-Mssallem, I. S. et al. Genome sequence of the date palm Phoenix dactylifera L. Nat. Commun. 4, 2274 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1345">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Xiao, Y. et al. The genome draft of coconut (Cocos nucifera). Gigascience 6, 1â11 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1348">24</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">GWAS mapping of the sex determination locus</h3><p>Increased assembly contiguity provides a unique resource for genome investigation and agricultural genomics research, including the ability to conduct GWAS mapping in date palms. To test the utility of our improved assembly, we used GWAS to map the sex determining region characterized previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Al-Dous, E. K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 521â527 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1360">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Torres, M. F. et al. Genus-wide sequencing supports a two-locus model for sex-determination in Phoenix. Nat. Commun. 9, 3969 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1363">17</a></sup>.</p><p>We characterized this trait in a GWAS panel comprised of date palms grown in two farms located in Al-Shuwaib, Abu Dhabi and Hamriyah, Ras Al-Khaimah, United Arab Emirates (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM5">1</a>). Together, they comprise a diverse mapping population of 145 female varieties and 12 male individuals without obvious populationÂ structure related to the two farms (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">3</a>, Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM5">1</a>). We used paired-end (2âÃâ100âbp) Illumina sequencing to re-sequence genomes of the 157 date palms to an average depth of ~11.3Ã (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM5">1</a>). Following application of SNP quality control filters, we identified 7,149,205 SNPs or approximately 9.24âSNPs/kb, in our GWAS panel. We performed compressed mixed linear model (CMLM) GWAS using both the first five principal components of population structure and kinship information as covariates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Lipka, A. E. et al. GAPIT: Genome association and prediction integrated tool. Bioinformatics 28, 2397â2399 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1382">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Zhang, Z. et al. Mixed linear model approach adapted for genome-wide association studies. Nat. Genet. 42, 355â360 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1385">26</a></sup> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig2">2a</a>; Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM6">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM7">3</a>). Since LD decays relatively rapidly in our GWAS panel (half decay distanceâ=â22.9âkb, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig2">2b</a>), we chose not to remove LD-correlated SNPs. Instead, we randomly sample 392,948 SNPs to yield ~1 SNP every 2âkb for mapping. We applied a conservative Bonferroni threshold to identify significant SNPs, and ensure that all GWAS models with significant associations had a genomic inflation factor Î» of 1âÂ±â0.15 in quantileâquantile plots.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12604-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12604_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12604-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12604_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="983" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>GWAS analysis of the sex determination region of date palm. <b>a</b> PCA of genetic structure in the GWAS panel. Points are colored based on whether the ancestry of the variety traces to Middle Eastern (blue) or North African (orange) date populations. The shapes of the points indicate the location of the tree in Al-ShuwaibÂ (triangle) or Ras Al-Khaimah (dot), <b>b</b> decay of linkage disequilibrium with physical distance in the GWAS mapping population. The half decay distance is 22.9âkb, <b>c</b> GWAS of sex determination in date palm (<i>n</i>â=â157 date palms). Top: Manhattan plot using the randomly downsampled SNP set (392,948 SNPs) for all linkage groups and unplaced scaffolds, below: Manhattan plot using the full SNP set on LG 12. Corresponding quantile-quantile plot can be found in Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">4</a>. Source data are provided as a Source Data file</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Using these criteria, we are able to map the date palm sex determination region to the distal end of LG 12 consistent with previous reports<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Al-Dous, E. K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 521â527 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1438">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Torres, M. F. et al. Genus-wide sequencing supports a two-locus model for sex-determination in Phoenix. Nat. Commun. 9, 3969 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1441">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Mathew, L. S. et al. A first genetic map of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera) reveals long-range genome structure conservation in the palms. BMC Genomics 15, 285 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1444">21</a></sup> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig2">2c</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">4</a>). The GWAS peak consists of 112 SNPs that span a region of ~6âMb of placed primary contig sequence on LG 12, and 43 additional significant SNPs located on unplaced contigs (Supplementary DatasÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM8">4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM9">5</a>). The large section of LG 12 associated with sex is consistent with reduced recombination within the sex determining region (and thus high LD). It should be noted that a high-resolution view of this region reveals two peaks (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig2">2c</a>), but it is unclear if these are independent peaks or represent a statistical or assembly artifact.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">GWAS mapping of date palm fruit traits</h3><p>Given our success in mapping the sex determination region, we conducted GWAS on 21 fruit traits, many of which distinguish commercial cultivars (e.g., fruit color and sugar composition) and are important in the evolutionary diversification of date palms (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM5">1</a>). We phenotyped two fruit size (length and weight), eight fruit color (seven color parameters and anthocyanin level), fruit moisture, four fruit sugar content (sucrose, glucose, fructose, and percentage of sucrose to total sugar) and six acid level (fumaric, citric, malic, succinic, oxalic, and tartaric acids) traits (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">5</a>). Our analysis reveals no significant associations for most traits, including fruit size and acid content, suggesting these traits may require significantly larger mapping populations to successfully identify genes underlying these traits (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">6</a>). However, mapping of fruit color and sugar composition reveal significant GWAS hits.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genetics of fruit color in date palms</h3><p>Date palm fruits vary in color from deep red to pale yellow in the fresh (khalal) and ripe (rutab) stages (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig3">3a</a>). To measure fruit color, we use the RGB color space model [measuring the strength of red (R), green (G), and blue (B)] from scanned images of fruits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="RodrÃ­guez, G. R. et al. Tomato Analyzer: a useful software application to collect accurate and detailed morphological and colorimetric data from two-dimensional objects. J. Vis. Exp. 37, 1956 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1492">27</a></sup>, and the Commission Internationale de LâEclairage (CIE) model, which measures lightness (L), red/green (a), and yellow/blue (b) strengths from photographs. Principal component (PC) analysis on all color-related measurements indicated separation on PC1 for red/yellow colors and PC2 for yellow intensity (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">7</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12604-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12604_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12604-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12604_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="647" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The genetics of fruit color variation in date palms. <b>a</b> Color variation in khalal stage fruits among date palm varieties. <b>b</b> Fruit color measured by a color index (<b>a</b>, <b>b</b>) as a function of anthocyanin level with points colored according to the RGB score of the fruit color. Each point represents the average of eight fruits per cultivar. <b>c</b> GWAS of fruit color in date palms (<i>n</i>â=â157). Top: Manhattan plot using the randomly down-sampled SNP set (392,948 SNPs) for all linkage groups and unplaced scaffolds. Below: Manhattan plot using the full SNP set on LG 4. The dotted lines in each panel represent the Bonferroni significance threshold. <b>d</b> Gene models of <i>VIR</i> alleles. The <i>VIR</i><sup>IM</sup> allele is shown with the <i>Ibn Majid</i> retrotransposon insertion which introduces a premature stop in exon 3 and <i>vir</i><sup><i>saf</i></sup> allele is shown with the start codon polymorphism caused by a G to A change. <b>e</b> Boxplot distributions of fruit color phenotypes by <i>Ibn Majid</i> genotype (<i>n</i>â=â145 date palms). Center line is the median, bounds of box represent the frst and third quartiles, the upper and lower whiskers extend from the hinge to the largest or smallest value, respectively, no further than 1.5 * IQR from the hinge (where IQR is the inter-quartile range, or distance between the first and third quartiles). <b>f</b> RNA-Seq analysis of <i>VIR</i> expression across fruit development in two varieties with red and yellow fruits. Each point is the mean of normalized expression counts across three or more replicates. Error bars represent s.e.m. <b>g</b> Variation in fruit colors by two-locus genotype. Loci are defined by the <i>Ibn Majid</i> and start codon mutations in <i>VIR</i>. Points in <b>e</b>â<b>g</b> are colored as in (<b>b</b>). Points in <b>g</b> are jittered randomly on the <i>x</i>-axis. The source data underlying Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM10">3</a>b, c, eâg are provided as a Source Data file</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To map fruit color, we focus on the ratio of a/b, where higher values represent greater red coloration and lower values indicate yellow (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig3">3b</a>). We find significant associations (<i>F</i>-test, <i>P</i>â&lt;â1.3âÃâ10<sup>â7</sup>) in a region on LG 4, and no other significant associations (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig3">3c</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">4</a>, Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM9">5</a>). A separate analysis using all SNPs on LG 4 indicate that the GWAS peak spans a region of ~20âkb from 24.052 to 24.073âMb (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig3">3c</a>). This region contains a TOC75-3 chloroplastic-like gene, a gene of unknown function, and the orthologue of the oil palm <i>VIRESCENS</i> (<i>VIR</i>) gene that encodes an R2R3-MYB transcription factor that underlies fruit color in oil palms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Singh, R. et al. The oil palm VIRESCENS gene controls fruit colour and encodes a R2R3-MYB. Nat. Commun. 5, 4106 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1633">28</a></sup> (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">10</a>). Mapping the R and G color levels from the RGB model, and a, b, and L parameters from the CIE system also yielded significant associations in the same region (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM9">5</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">8</a>). Our GWAS approach therefore confirms our previous candidate gene analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Hazzouri, K. M. et al. Whole genome re-sequencing of date palms yields insights into diversification of a fruit tree crop. Nat. Commun. 6, 8824 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1647">13</a></sup> that suggested that the ortholog to oil palm <i>VIR</i> controls the red/yellow fruit color polymorphism in date palms.</p><p>We previously characterized the date palm <i>VIR</i> gene and reported a retrotransposon insertion polymorphism that interrupts exon 3 and introduces a premature stop codon at position 169 of the translated protein<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Hazzouri, K. M. et al. Whole genome re-sequencing of date palms yields insights into diversification of a fruit tree crop. Nat. Commun. 6, 8824 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1660">13</a></sup>. Examination of the BC4 male genome indicates our genome assembly also contains this retrotransposon insertion allele (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig3">3d</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">11</a>). However, in contrast to an earlier draft genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Al-Mssallem, I. S. et al. Genome sequence of the date palm Phoenix dactylifera L. Nat. Commun. 4, 2274 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1670">15</a></sup>, the long-read assembly contains a complete copy of the <i>copia</i>-like retrotransposon that is ~11.7âkb in length, has 469-bp long terminal repeats, has a 5-bp target site duplication at its insertion site, and flanked by sequences homologous to exon 3 of oil palm <i>VIR</i> on each side of the insertion (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig3">3d</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">11</a>). This retrotransposon, which we name <i>Ibn Majid</i>, after the 15th-century Arab navigator<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Tibbetts, G. R. Arab Navigation in the Indian Ocean Before the Coming of the Portuguese. Oriental Translation Fund, New Series, Vol. XLII (1981)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1690">29</a></sup>, is found in low copy numbers in the date palm genome, and shows only weak similarity to other plant <i>copia</i>-like retrotransposons.</p><p>We genotyped the retrotransposon insertion polymorphism in our GWAS panel and evaluate fruit color and anthocyanin content in each genotypic class. We observe that homozygotes for the <i>Ibn Majid</i> retrotransposon allele, hereafter <i>VIR</i><sup>IM</sup> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig3">3d</a>), invariably have yellow fruits (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig3">3e</a>). In contrast, homozygotes for the wild type allele (<i>VIR</i><sup>+</sup>) have predominantly red fruit, while heterozygous varieties have yellow fruits or fruits with intermediate colors including shades of orange. The fruit color patterns are consistent with what we observe in anthocyanin content: <i>VIR</i><sup>IM</sup>/<i>VIR</i><sup>IM</sup> homozygotes produce undetectable amounts of anthocyanin, <i>VIR</i><sup>+</sup>/<i>VIR</i><sup>+</sup> have predominantly high concentrations, and <i>VIR</i><sup>+</sup>/<i>VIR</i><sup>IM</sup> produce undetectable or trace amounts of anthocyanin (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">9</a>). This suggests that <i>Ibn Majid</i> disrupts an activator of anthocyanin biosynthesis similar to oil palm truncation alleles at the <i>VIR</i> locus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Singh, R. et al. The oil palm VIRESCENS gene controls fruit colour and encodes a R2R3-MYB. Nat. Commun. 5, 4106 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1754">28</a></sup>. While anthocyanin content in these genotypic classes are consistent with functional variation at <i>VIR</i>, GWAS on anthocyanin content does not lead to significant hits, possibly due to greater variability of anthocyanin level measurements that result in reduced power to detect GWAS peaks.</p><p>Analysis of fruit color phenotypes of putative <i>VIR</i><sup>+</sup>/<i>VIR</i><sup>+</sup> homozygotes revealed four varieties that produce yellow fruits despite the absence of the retrotransposon insertion (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig3">3e</a>). We observe that these four varieties, including the common Saudi Arabian variety Safri (=Sufri), are homozygous for a SNP that changes the predicted translation initiation codon of <i>VIR</i> from ATG to ATA (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig3">3d</a>) at position 24,051,180 of LG 4 in the BC4 male assembly (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">11</a>). No other in-frame methionine codons are found immediately upstream or downstream of this position in <i>VIR</i><sup>+</sup> suggesting no alternate translation initiation sites. This suggests thatÂ the loss of a translation initiation codon likely results in a loss-of-function allele, hereafter <i>vir</i><sup>saf</sup>, at the <i>VIR</i> locus.</p><p>We collected RNA-Seq data to determine how wild type and <i>VIR</i><sup>IM</sup> alleles are expressed during fruit development. RNA-Seq analysis reveals that date palm <i>VIR</i> is expressed late in developing fruits, peaking at 120â135 days post pollination (dpp) in cultivars with both red (Khenezi variety) and yellow (Khalas) fruits (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig3">3f</a>). Our analysis confirms that <i>VIR</i><sup>IM</sup> is expressed and that Khalas has reduced expression compared with Khenezi at 105, 120, and 135 days post pollination (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">12</a>).</p><p>The red color of <i>VIR</i><sup>+</sup>/<i>vir</i><sup>saf</sup> fruits implies that <i>VIR</i><sup>+</sup> is haplosufficient, and <i>vir</i><sup>saf</sup> is recessive consistent with it being a loss-of-function allele (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig3">3g</a>). In contrast, <i>VIR</i><sup>+</sup>/<i>VIR</i><sup>IM</sup> heterozygotes have yellow or orange fruits in which <i>VIR</i><sup>IM</sup> acts as a dominant, or possibly semidominant, negative inhibitor that interferes with the expression of the wild-type <i>VIR</i><sup>+</sup> allele. This genetic behavior is similar to mutants of other R2R3-MYB transcription factors such as maize <i>C1</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Goff, S. A., Cone, K. C. &amp; Fromm, M. E. Identification of functional domains in the maize transcriptional activator C1: comparison of wild-type and dominant inhibitor proteins. Genes Dev. 5, 298â309 (1991)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1860">30</a></sup>. Thus red fruits are produced by <i>VIR</i><sup>+</sup>/<i>VIR</i><sup>+</sup> and <i>VIR</i><sup>+</sup>/<i>vir</i><sup>saf</sup>, yellow fruits by <i>vir</i><sup>saf</sup>/<i>vir</i><sup>saf</sup>, <i>VIR</i><sup>IM</sup>/<i>VIR</i><sup>IM</sup>, <i>VIR</i><sup>IM</sup>/<i>vir</i><sup>saf</sup>, and some <i>VIR</i><sup>+</sup>/<i>VIR</i><sup>IM</sup> genotypes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig3">3g</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">10</a>) and intermediate colors by <i>VIR</i><sup>+</sup>/<i>VIR</i><sup>IM</sup> genotypes, with the action of the <i>VIR</i><sup>+</sup>/<i>VIR</i><sup>IM</sup> genotypes due to dominant negative inhibition of anthocyanin production.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genetics of fruit sugar composition</h3><p>Sugar composition is a characteristic of date fruits that varies among cultivars and contributes to the distinctive flavor profile of individual varieties. During kimri and khalal stages of fruit development, sucrose and starch accumulate in the mesocarp of developing date fruits. As the fruit matures, sucrose is hydrolyzed into the reducing sugars fructose and glucose to varying degrees, dependent on the variety. Relative concentrations of sucrose, fructose and glucose are used to classify date palm varieties as either sucrose type with high concentrations of sucrose at the tamar stage (the stage when date fruits have low-moisture content and are typically consumed) or reducing-sugar type defined by high concentrations of glucose and fructose<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Dowson, V. H. W. &amp; Aten, A. Dates, handling, processing and packing. (Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations, Rome, 1962)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d10202e1948">31</a></sup> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig4">4a, b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12604-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12604_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12604-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12604_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="766" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Date palm fruit sugar composition phenotypes in the GWAS panel. <b>a</b> Sugar composition (g/100âg dry matter) of reducing-type and sucrose-type varieties Khalas and Soukar Iraqi at the tamar stage (Sucâ=âsucrose, Fruâ=âfructose, Gluâ=âglucose). <b>b</b> Distribution of the percentage sucrose to total sugar in the mapping population. <b>c</b> Correlations of sugar concentrations across varieties. Source data are provided as a Source Data file</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We quantified sucrose, glucose, and fructose at tamar stage fruits, and observed a wide range of sugar composition across varieties. Most samples, including the Khalas variety, are of the reducing-sugar type with high concentrations of glucose and fructose and a low percentage of sucrose to total sugar ([sucrose]/[glucoseâ+âfructoseâ+âsucrose]) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig4">4a, b</a>). Sucrose-type varieties with high sucrose concentrations are less common and include the highly prized Sukkari (=Soukar/Soukari which in English is translated as sweet) varieties (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig4">4a, b</a>). We find concentrations of these three sugars are strongly correlated, with sucroseâglucose, sucroseâfructose, and sucroseâ(glucoseâ+âfructose) being strongly negatively correlated (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig4">4c</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">13</a>). Thus, date palm genotypes with increased amounts of sucrose also have decreased levels of both glucose and fructose.</p><p>We mapped levels of sucrose, glucose, and fructose and percent sucrose to total sugar [sucrose]/[sucroseâ+âglucoseâ+âfructose], and found all four traits share a common GWAS peak on LG 14 (<i>F</i>-test, <i>P</i>â&lt;â1.3âÃâ10<sup>â7</sup>) [Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig5">5a</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">11</a>, Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM9">5</a>]. Separate analysis restricted to SNPs on LG 14 indicates that SNPs significantly associated with these traits span a broad region of ~1.1âMb from approximately positions 2.4 to 3.6âMbÂ (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig5">5a</a>). Of the 162 annotated protein-coding genes in this region in the BC4 male assembly (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM10">6</a>), three encode invertase (Î²-fructofuranosidase) enzymes, which function to hydrolyze sucrose into fructose and glucose and function in sugar accumulation in fruits from a range of species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Sturm, A., Hess, D., Lee, H. S. &amp; Lienhard, S. Neutral invertase is a novel type of sucrose-cleaving enzyme. Physiol. Plant. 107, 159â165 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2024">32</a></sup>. The positional invertase candidates include an alkaline/neutral invertase (<i>A/N-INV1</i>; gene ID chr14G0028200) located near the center of the GWAS peak at 3.087âMb of the LG 14 assembly (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig5">5a</a>, Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM10">6</a>), and two adjacently-linked cell wall invertases (<i>CWINV1</i> and <i>CWINV3</i>, gene ID chr14G0022900 and chr13G0023100, respectively) and located at the 5â² end of the GWAS region at approximately 2.469 and 2.512âMb (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig5">5a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12604-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12604_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12604-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12604_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="598" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>GWAS mapping of sugar composition in date palm fruit and functional characterization of candidate genes. <b>a</b> Top: Manhattan plot GWAS results for tamar stage fruit sugar composition measured as [sucrose]/[sucroseâ+âglucoseâ+âfructose]. The plot is based on analysis conducted with the randomly downsampled SNP call set. Middle: Manhattan plot of the region on LG 14 containing significant associations based on analysis of all SNPs on this linkage group. Dotted lines represent the Bonferroni significance threshold. Gray vertical lines represent boundaries of the regions highlighted below. Bottom: Close-up of two highlighted regions with invertase genes in the vicinity of the GWAS peak. From top to bottom, gene models, normalized coverage depth in 500âbp windows for a sucrose-type variety (Soukar Iraqi), a reducing sugar-type variety (Khalas), and a heatmap of normalized coverage depth (low coverage is dark blue to high coverage light blue; zero coverageâ=âgray) of the ten most extreme sugar composition phenotypes at each end of the sucrose-type and reducing-type distributions (see Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">13</a> for all samples). Numbers in parenthesis next to variety names are the sucrose to total sugar rank among the 125 varieties with sugar measurements, where (1) is highest sucrose. <b>b</b> Expression of three candidate invertase genes in developing fruit at different stages post pollination (meanâÂ±âs.e.m.) in the Khenezi variety. <b>c</b>â<b>e</b> Differential gene expression of khalal stage fruit between four sucrose-type and four reducing-type varieties at three candidate invertase genes (meanâÂ±âs.e.m.). <b>f</b> Invertase enzyme activity in khalal stage fruit in two reducing-type and two sucrose-type varieties (meanâÂ±âs.d.). Source data are provided as a Source Data file</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The CWINV1 and CWINV3 proteins show 98.58% amino acid identity, suggesting these are tandem duplicates of each other. We check for additional un-annotated copies of invertase in this region by aligning predicted transcripts for each of the three genes to this region, and recovered a minus strand sequence (which we refer to as <i>CWINV2</i>), with close homology to <i>CWINV1</i> and <i>CWINV3</i>, and located between these two genes at positions 2,489,373 to 2,485,592 on LG14 (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig5">5a</a>). However, this coding region has multiple frame-shift mutations in our PacBio assembly and may be a pseudogene in the BC4 male individual. Examination of the whole genome re-sequencing data suggests that many of these frameshift mutations in <i>CWINV2</i> are not present in other date palm samples, and it is unclear if this gene is functional in at least some varieties. Nevertheless, we do not consider <i>CWINV2</i> further in our analyses, although we note that the functional status of this locus does not materially affect our conclusions (see below).</p><p>We scanned the fruit sugar composition GWAS region for candidate major effect SNPs, small coding insertion/deletion (indel) polymorphisms and copy number variants that might explain sugar composition variation. A sliding window analysis of normalized coverage depth in this region found a polymorphic deletion of ~40âkb at approximately 2.467 to 2.507âMb that includes the 5â² half of <i>CWINV1</i> (including the first two coding exons and its promoter region), a region ~5âkb downstream of <i>CWINV3</i>, and the putative <i>CWINV2</i> pseudogene (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig5">5a</a>). The extent of this deletion renders <i>CWINV1</i> nonfunctional.
