A second polymorphic deletion of ~5âkb is located closer to the center of the GWAS peak between approximately 3.088 and 3.093âMb in noncoding sequence between the 5â² end of <i>A/N-INV1</i> and an adjacent histone deacetylase (HDA) gene (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig5">5a</a>).</p><p>Examination of coverage depth data in the <i>CWINV1/3</i> and <i>A/N-INV1</i> deletion regions suggests that sucrose-type varieties, such as Soukar Iraqi, are more frequently homozygous for these deletions than reducing-type varieties such as Khalas (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig5">5a</a>, Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">12</a><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1"> and 13</a>). We find that homozygotes for the deletion at <i>CWINV1/3</i> have higher sucrose concentration than alternate genotypes (one-sided Wilcoxon-rank sum test, <i>W</i>â=â1264, <i>P</i>â&lt;â9.3âÃâ10<sup>â7</sup>) as do homozygotes for the deletion in the promoter region of <i>A/N-INV1</i> (<i>W</i>â=â855<i>, P</i>â&lt;â3.8âÃâ10<sup>â5</sup>).</p><p>RNA-Seq analysis of invertase genes across date palm fruit development shows that all three invertases are expressed in the fruit and that <i>A/N-INV1</i> peaks in expression at ~105 days post pollination, whereas <i>CWINV1</i> and <i>CWINV3</i> peak at ~120 days (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig5">5b</a>). Tests of RNA levels between khalal stage fruit (ca. 120 days after pollination) of sucrose- and reducing-type varieties indicate that both <i>CWINV1</i> (Wald test, <i>P</i>â&lt;â1.25âÃâ10<sup>â16</sup>) and <i>CWINV3</i> (<i>P</i>â&lt;â3.06âÃâ10<sup>â19</sup>) are differentially expressed (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig5">5c, d</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">14</a>). Log<sub>2</sub> fold-changes between sucrose- and reducing-type varieties are 2.69 and 4.23 for <i>CWINV1</i> and <i>CWINV3</i>, with higher expression in reducing-types suggesting a large difference in gene expression in developing fruits (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">14</a>). Reduced expression in both <i>CWINV1</i> and <i>CWINV3</i> is consistent with the 40-kb deletion removing much of <i>CWINV1</i>, including its promoter region and possibly affecting a 3â² regulatory region of <i>CWINV3</i>, although we cannot exclude the possibility that there is additional functional variation that alters the regulation of this gene. In contrast, <i>A/N-INV1</i> shows a much weaker difference in expression between fruit types (Wald test, <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.08, log2 fold-changeâ=â0.41, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig5">5e</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">14</a>). Finally, a test of differences in invertase enzyme activity between the two sugar types find that sucrose-type varieties have significantly lower activity (two-sided <i>t</i> test, <i>t</i>â=â15.001, d<i>f</i>â=â2, <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.0044) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig5">5f</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">15</a>) at this stage as reported previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Hasegawa, S. &amp; Smolensky, D. C. Date invertase: properties and activity associated with maturation and quality. J. Agric. Food Chem. 18, 902â904 (1970)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2271">33</a></sup>.</p><p>The GWAS association in a region with multiple invertase genes, prevalence of invertase gene deletions in sucrose-type varieties, and negative correlation between sucrose and hexose sugars across varieties (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig4">4c</a>, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig5">5</a>, Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM10">6</a>), are consistent with fruit sugar composition being controlled by functional variation at one or more invertase genes. These observations support a model where gene-deletion polymorphisms and/or altered gene regulatory regions reduce expression of one or more fruit-expressed invertases, which limits sucrose hydrolysis at advanced stages of development of sucrose-type fruits. It remains unclear whether only the cell wall invertases (<i>CWINV1</i>, <i>CWINV3</i>), the alkaline/neutral invertase (<i>A/N-INV1</i>), or both control sugar composition in date palm fruit. Moreover, we are uncertain ifÂ the <i>CWINV2</i> pseudogene also impacts this trait (e.g., if it is functional in some varieties), but we note that this locus is found in the middle of the 40-kb deletion that spans the putative regulatory regions of <i>CWINV1</i> and <i>3</i> and deletes coding exons of <i>CWINV1</i>. Thus, its functional status does not affect our hypothesis that this deletion is the major candidate causal polymorphism. We also note that the cell wall invertases and the <i>A/N-INV1</i> genes are ~600âkb apart, which may explain the wide (~1âMb) GWAS peak for fruit sugar composition despite the outcrossing nature of date palms, and suggests that both cell wall and alkaline/neutral invertase types contribute to variation in this trait.</p><p>Finally, while these deletions are clearly correlated with both sucrose/reducing sugar and invertase transcript levels, the correlations are not perfect; indeed, some sucrose-type varieties do not contain the <i>CWINV1/3</i> deletion and some of the reducing sugar varieties contain the <i>A/N-INV1-</i>associated deletion. This suggests that either there are other, as yet unidentified, causal polymorphisms (such as seen in the independent start codon mutation in the <i>vir</i><sup>saf</sup> allele and fruit color), or other loci may be contributing to this quantitative trait. Thus, although our data provides strong support for one or more invertases on LG 14 being responsible for sugar composition in date palm fruits, and that large deletions at these loci are associated with reduced invertase activity, further molecular genetic study will be needed to fully dissect the functional underpinnings of this trait.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>Date palms are a key crop of the Middle East and North Africa, and are a mainstay of arid land agriculture. Despite its economic and cultural importance, genetic studies of date palm are limited by relatively few genomic resources and challenges posed by working with tree species with long generation times. To help remedy this situation, our long-read sequencing approach has produced an improved genome assembly for date palms that is 18% larger and is more contiguous than two current draft reference genome assemblies based on short-read sequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Al-Dous, E. K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 521â527 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2334">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Al-Mssallem, I. S. et al. Genome sequence of the date palm Phoenix dactylifera L. Nat. Commun. 4, 2274 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2337">15</a></sup>. This long-read genome sequence assembly, coupled with access to two mature date palm orchards in the United Arab Emirates with 157 male and female individuals, has allowed us to conduct genome-wide association mapping in this species.</p><p>We successfully mapped the previously identified sex determination locus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Al-Dous, E. K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 521â527 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2344">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Torres, M. F. et al. Genus-wide sequencing supports a two-locus model for sex-determination in Phoenix. Nat. Commun. 9, 3969 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2347">17</a></sup>, as well as genes for both fruit color and sugar level polymorphisms. However, our relatively small GWAS panel of 145 female samples allowed us to map only 11 of 21 fruit traits, many of which were correlated with each other. If phenotypic variation in the unmapped traits are genetically determined, future mapping efforts will require significantly larger mapping populations, which may be difficult in the near term given we currently do not know any date palm orchard in the world with larger numbers of different date palm cultivars than the one we used in this study.</p><p>Our results support the idea that key domestication and diversification traits are driven by evolutionary convergence in homologous genes across unrelated taxa. We find that fruit color polymorphism in date palms is caused by mutations in a gene encoding an R2R3-MYB transcription factor. Homologous genes in grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Walker, A. R. et al. White grapes arose through the mutation of two similar and adjacent regulatory genes. Plant J. 49, 772â785 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2354">34</a></sup>, apple<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Zhang, L. et al. A high-quality apple genome assembly reveals the association of a retrotransposon and red fruit colour. Nat. Commun. 10, 1494 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2358">35</a></sup>, cacao<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Motamayor, J. C. et al. The genome sequence of the most widely cultivated cacao type and its use to identify candidate genes regulating pod color. Genome Biol. 14, r53 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2362">36</a></sup>, citrus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Huang, D. et al. Subfunctionalization of the Ruby2âRuby1 gene cluster during the domestication of citrus. Nat. Plants 4, 930â941 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2366">37</a></sup>, and other perennial crops across different plant families are also responsible for fruit color variation in those species. We have also mapped fruit sugar content to a genomic region that includes invertases that segregate deletion polymorphisms in the date palm population. The cell wall invertase genes show large expression differences between varieties with different fruit sugar compositions, suggesting these may be play a causal role in this trait. Natural variation of invertase is also implicated in sugar composition of tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Klann, E. M., Chetelat, R. T. &amp; Bennett, A. B. Expression of acid invertase gene controls sugar composition in tomato (Lycopersicon) fruit. Plant Physiol. 103, 863â870 (1993)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2370">38</a></sup> and grapes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Davies, C. &amp; Robinson, S. P. Sugar accumulation in grape berries. Cloning of two putative vacuolar invertase cDNAs and their expression in grapevine tissues. Plant Physiol. 111, 275â283 (1996)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2375">39</a></sup> and suggests an important role for parallel evolution during diversification of domesticated fruit crops. Interestingly, for both fruit color and sugar composition, structural mutations (a retrotransposon insertion in <i>VIR</i> and kilobase-sized deletions in the invertase genes) appear to be primarily responsible for phenotypic changes.</p><p>Our results support previous studies which show that phenotypic convergence/parallelism is a hallmark of crop domestication, a result of similar selection pressures acting on different crop species across distinct human cultures<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Purugganan, M. D. &amp; Fuller, D. Q. The nature of selection during plant domestication. Nature 457, 843â848 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2385">40</a></sup>. Indeed, the parallel evolution of traits among domesticated species was noted by N. I. Vavilov, who in 1922 proposed the genetic law of homologous series of variation among related crop species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Vavilov, N. I. The law of homologous series in variation. J. Genet. 12, 47â89 (1922)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2389">41</a></sup>. As we continue to uncover the genetic basis for crop evolution, we may compare and contrast genetic architectures across the entire spectrum of domesticated taxa, and discover similarities in the evolutionary trajectories even between annual and perennial crops despite their marked differences in life history.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Genome sequencing of the BC4 male</h3><p>We sampled a backcross male date palm located at the United States Department of Agriculture (USDA)/University of California, Riverside farm in Thermal, California (USDA accession No. PI 555415, Source RIV 7545 PL). This male was produced by four generations of backcrossing with a Barhee female as the recurrent parent as part of a breeding program at the USDA, USA that was discontinued in the 1970s<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Krueger, R. R. Date palm germplasm: overview and utilization in the USA. In Proc. International Conference on Date Palms (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2405">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Nixon, R. W. &amp; Furr, J. R. Problems and Progress In Date Breeding. (Coachella, 1965)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2408">18</a></sup>. Leaflets were cleaned and snap frozen on liquid nitrogen prior to transport to the Arizona Genomics Institute (University of Arizona, Tucson, AZ) for extraction of high molecular weight DNA and sequencing.</p><p>The genome of the BC4 male was sequenced using a PacBio RSII sequencing platform. High molecular weight DNA for sequencing was extracted from young leaves adopting the protocol of Doyle and Doyle<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Doyle, J. J. &amp; Doyle, J. L. A rapid DNA isolation procedure for small quantities of fresh leaf tissue. Phytochem. Bull. 19, 11â15 (1987)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2415">42</a></sup> with minor modifications. PacBio library preparation followed the 20âkb protocol [<a href="http://www.pacb.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/User-Bulletin-Guidelines-for-Preparing-20-kb-SMRTbell-Templates.pdf">http://www.pacb.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/User-Bulletin-Guidelines-for-Preparing-20-kb-SMRTbell-Templates.pdf</a>] and three libraries (gel-selected at 20, 25, and 30âkb) were built. Eighty-five SMRT cells were sequenced on a RSII sequencer with movie collection time of 6âh. About 6.4 million reads were generated, totaling 72âGb of data (mean subread length 11.2âkb, N50 18.5âkb). Additional sequencing of a short insert library (2âÃâ100âbp paired-end) was conducted with an Illumina HiSeq 2500 sequencer.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genome assembly</h3><p>We did a k-mer-based estimation of the genome size from raw short read sequences of the BC4 male genome for assembly purposes (KmerFreq_AR in SOAPdenovo2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2434">43</a></sup>) with default settings and k-mer length set to 17. Note that an experimental genome size estimate for <i>P. dactylifera</i> was also done using flow cytometry (see below). PacBio reads were then assembled with FALCON-Unzip<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Chin, C.-S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2441">19</a></sup> (v. falcon-2017.06.28â18.01-py2.7-ucs2) with a seed coverage of 55Ã and the k-mer-based genome size estimate of 774âMb as input. The Unzip module was run with default settings.</p><p>The resulting assembly was polished by aligning raw PacBio reads with Quiver and Arrow (part of the SMRT Analysis suite v. 2.3.0) followed by running Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: An integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS One 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2448">44</a></sup> v. 1.18 with Illumina short read sequences from the BC4 male. Inputs to Pilon were produced by trimming the short reads with Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114â2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2452">45</a></sup> (v. 0.32) to remove 3â² bases below base quality of Q30 and reads shorter than 30 nucleotides. Reads were then aligned to the output of Arrow with Bowtie2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods 9, 357â359 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2456">46</a></sup> (v. 2.2.6).</p><p>The polished primary contigs were anchored to LGs of the existing genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Mathew, L. S. et al. A first genetic map of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera) reveals long-range genome structure conservation in the palms. BMC Genomics 15, 285 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2463">21</a></sup> with ALLMAPS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Tang, H. et al. ALLMAPS: robust scaffold ordering based on multiple maps. Genome Biol. 16, 3 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2467">22</a></sup> to produce an anchored haploid assembly. Scaffold sequences for the genetic map were obtained from <a href="http://qatar-weill.cornell.edu/research/datepalmGenome/edition3/PdactyKAsm30_r20101206.fasta.gz">http://qatar-weill.cornell.edu/research/datepalmGenome/edition3/PdactyKAsm30_r20101206.fasta.gz</a>. Upon alignment to the genetic map and after manual inspection of the realignment of the raw reads to the assembly, we found only one instance of mis-assembly: one contig had to be split since two contig ends were merged head-to-head.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genome annotation</h3><p>We generated RNA-Seq libraries from multiple khalal stage fruit (see below), a mixture of male and female flower buds (referred to as âflowerâ below), and pollen, and conducted 2âÃâ100âbp paired-end sequencing on an Illumina HiSeq 2500 instrument (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">7</a>). Additional date palm RNA-Seq data from leaf and root were downloaded from the Sequence Read Archive [<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra</a>] (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">7</a>). RNA-Seq reads were trimmed with Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114â2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2499">45</a></sup>, aligned to the haploid assembly with STAR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Dobin, A. et al. STAR: ultrafast universal RNA-seq aligner. Bioinformatics 29, 15â21 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2503">47</a></sup> (v.2.4.0.1), and gene models predicted by StringTie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Pertea, M., Kim, D., Pertea, G. M., Leek, J. T. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Transcript-level expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with HISAT, StringTie and Ballgown. Nat. Protoc. 11, 1650â1667 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2508">48</a></sup> (v. 1.3.2) to be used as training for Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics 19(Suppl 2), ii215âii225 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2512">49</a></sup> (v. 2.3).</p><p>Gene annotation was performed using the MAKER2 pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513â524 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2519">50</a></sup> (v. 2.31). Homology-based evidence, included 7097 ESTs (downloaded from NCBI EST database on February 9, 2017), protein sequences from Uniprot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="The UniProt Consortium. UniProt: a hub for protein information. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D204âD212 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2523">51</a></sup>, a date palm proteome [<a href="http://qatar-weill.cornell.edu/research/research-highlights/date-palm-research-program/date-palm-genome-data">http://qatar-weill.cornell.edu/research/research-highlights/date-palm-research-program/date-palm-genome-data</a>], an oil palm proteome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Low, E. T. L. et al. Analyses of hypomethylated oil palm gene space. PLoS One 9, e86728 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2534">52</a></sup>, and the RNA-Seq derived models from above. Ab initio prediction was performed with Augustus (v. 3.0) trained as described in Bowman et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Bowman, M. J., Pulman, J. A., Liu, T. L. &amp; Childs, K. L. A modified GC-specific MAKER gene annotation method reveals improved and novel gene predictions of high and low GC content in Oryza sativa. BMC Bioinforma. 18, 522 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2538">53</a></sup> with gene models produced with StringTie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Pertea, M., Kim, D., Pertea, G. M., Leek, J. T. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Transcript-level expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with HISAT, StringTie and Ballgown. Nat. Protoc. 11, 1650â1667 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2543">48</a></sup> (v. 1.3.2), from the RNA-Seq alignments.</p><p>The raw MAKER2 annotation was parsed, removing models containing TE domains and lacking evidence of transcription or the presence of a Pfam domain as described in Bowman et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Bowman, M. J., Pulman, J. A., Liu, T. L. &amp; Childs, K. L. A modified GC-specific MAKER gene annotation method reveals improved and novel gene predictions of high and low GC content in Oryza sativa. BMC Bioinforma. 18, 522 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2550">53</a></sup>. With about 1Ã of non-organellar single-end WGS Illumina reads, a de novo (non assembly-based) repeat library was produced with RepeatExplorer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="NovÃ¡k, P., Neumann, P., Pech, J., Steinhaisl, J. &amp; MacAs, J. RepeatExplorer: a galaxy-based web server for genome-wide characterization of eukaryotic repetitive elements from next-generation sequence reads. Bioinformatics 29, 792â793 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2554">54</a></sup>, and parsed as in Copetti et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Copetti, D. et al. RiTE database: a resource database for genus-wide rice genomics and evolutionary biology. BMC Genomics 16, 538 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2558">55</a></sup>. Repeat annotation of the assembly was performed with RepeatMasker (v. 4.0.6; [<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>] in nucleotide space) and Blaster<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Flutre, T., Duprat, E., Feuillet, C. &amp; Quesneville, H. Considering transposable element diversification in de novo annotation approaches. PLoS One 6, e16526 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2569">56</a></sup> (part of the REPET v 2.5 package, in protein space) and later reconciled in a single annotation file. Noncoding RNAs were predicted with Infernal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Nawrocki, E. P. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.1: 100-fold faster RNA homology searches. Bioinformatics 29, 2933â2935 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2574">57</a></sup> (v. 1.1.2) with the Rfam library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Nawrocki, E. P. et al. Rfam 12.0: Updates to the RNA families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D130âD137 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2578">58</a></sup> (v. 12.2). Hits above the <i>e</i>-value threshold of 1âÃâ10<sup>â5</sup> were filtered out, as well as results with score lower than the family specific gathering threshold. When loci on both strands were predicted, only the hit with the highest score was kept. Transfer RNAs were also predicted using tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Schattner, P., Brooks, A. N. &amp; Lowe, T. M. The tRNAscan-SE, snoscan and snoGPS web servers for the detection of tRNAs and snoRNAs. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W686âW689 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2587">59</a></sup> (v. 2.0) with default parameters.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genome quality assessment</h3><p>Visualizations of the genome assembly were produced with assembly-stats software (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">1</a>, [<a href="https://assembly-stats.readme.io/docs">https://assembly-stats.readme.io/docs</a>]). Assembly completeness was evaluated by characterizing the gene space with BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: Assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2609">20</a></sup> using 1440 plant ortholog groups (v. 3) and by aligning ESTs to the diploid assembly with Blat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Kent, W. J. BLATâThe BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656â664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2613">60</a></sup> (v. 350).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Date palm genome size estimation</h3><p>The genome size was estimated using the one-step flow cytometry procedure described in DoleÅ¾el et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="DoleÅ¾el, J., Greilhuber, J. &amp; Suda, J. Estimation of nuclear DNA content in plants using flow cytometry. Nat. Protoc. 2, 2233â2244 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2626">61</a></sup> with slight modifications. Briefly, approximately 1âcm<sup>2</sup> of leaf material from two <i>P. dactylifera</i> samples at the Royal Botanic Gardens, Kew, UK collection was incubated for 30âs on ice in 1âml of âgeneral purpose bufferâ (GPB)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Loureiro, J., Rodriguez, E., DoleÅ¾el, J. &amp; Santos, C. Two new nuclear isolation buffers for plant DNA flow cytometry: a test with 37 species. Ann. Bot. 100, 875â888 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2635">62</a></sup> supplemented with 3% PVP-40 to soften the leaf. Then a similar amount of leaf material of the calibration standard <i>Petroselinum crispum</i> (Mill.) Fuss (1C valueâ=â2201âMb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Obermayer, R., Leitch, I. J., Hanson, L. &amp; Bennett, M. D. Nuclear DNA C-values in 30 species double the familial representation in pteridophytes. Ann. Bot. 90, 209â217 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2643">63</a></sup> was added and the combined material was chopped rapidly (but not too vigorously) using a new razor blade. A further 1âml of the GPB buffer was added and then the homogenate was filtered through a 30âÂµm nylon mesh (Celltrics 30âÂµM mesh, Sysmex, Goritz, Germany) into a tube, 100âÎ¼l propidium iodide (1âmg/mL) was added, and the sample was incubated on ice for 10âmin. The relative fluorescence of 5000 particles was recorded using a Partec Cyflow SL3 flow cytometer (Partec GmbH, MÃ¼nster, Germany) fitted with a 100âmW green solid-state laser (532ânm, Cobolt Samba, Solna, Sweden). Three replicates of each leaf were processed, and the output histograms were analyzed using the FlowMax software v.2.4 (Partec GmbH). The 1C value of <i>P. dactylifera</i> (Mbp) was calculated as: (Mean peak position of <i>P. dactylifera</i>/Mean peak position of <i>P. crispum</i>)âÃâ2201âMb (=1C value of <i>P. crispum</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Obermayer, R., Leitch, I. J., Hanson, L. &amp; Bennett, M. D. Nuclear DNA C-values in 30 species double the familial representation in pteridophytes. Ann. Bot. 90, 209â217 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2659">63</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">GWAS panel</h3><p>Phenotyping for the GWAS was conducted on date palm trees located on two farms in the United Arab Emirates. The farms are located at The Date Palm Research Center in Hamriyah, Ras Al-Khaimah (<i>n</i>â=â46) [latitude:25.60859749156817, longitude: 55.93000173568726] and in Al-Shuwaib, Al-Ain, Abu Dhabi (<i>n</i>â=â111) [latitude: 24.771976901905425, longitude: 55.812156200408936]. The population consists primarily of female commercial varieties (<i>n</i>â=â145). Males (<i>n</i>â=â12) growing on the farms were also sequenced primarily for the purpose of mapping the sex determining locus.</p><p>Khalal stage fruit samples were collected from spring to fall in 2016, and either snap frozen on liquid nitrogen for RNA-sequencing or collected as fresh fruits for photography, scanning (see below) and characterization of other fruit traits. Tamar stage fruits from the same trees were collected in summer 2017 for sugar and organic acid profiling. Leaf samples were collected for DNA extraction and genome sequencing.</p><p>Genomic DNA was extracted from either leaf or fruit mesocarp/epicarp tissue using plant DNeasy mini kit (Qiagen, Venlo, Netherlands). DNA extraction columns, and libraries prepared using Illumina Nextera (San Diego, CA) kit. A 2âÃâ100âbp paired-end sequencing was conducted on an Illumina HiSeq 2500 sequencer with up to eight libraries per lane. Reads were demultiplexed and those passing Illumina quality control filters were processed with Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114â2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2689">45</a></sup> (v. 0.36) to remove contaminating adapter sequences. For adapter removal, we used the adapter and Nextera transposase sequence database included with the Trimmomatic (v. 0.32) download with the following setting ILLUMINACLIP:ãadapter libraryã:2:30:10 MINLEN:76 to retain only read pairs where both reads were 76âbps or longer following trimming.</p><p>Reads were aligned to the unmasked BC4 male assembly (primary contigs only) using bwa mem (v. 0.7.15-r1140 [<a href="http://bio-bwa.sourceforge.net">http://bio-bwa.sourceforge.net</a>]). The bwa mem aligner was run with the -M option to mark supplementary reads (0âÃâ800 bitwise flag) as secondary (0âÃâ100). Sample alignments were processed with FixMateInformation (Picard-tools v. 2.8.2; <a href="http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard">http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard</a>) to ensure consistency in paired-read information, SamSort (Picard-tools v. 2.8.2) to coordinate-sort the alignments, MarkDuplicates (Picard-tools v. 2.8.2) to flag duplicate read pairs, and with GATK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Depristo, M. A. et al. A framework for variation discovery and genotyping using next-generation DNA sequencing data. Nat. Genet. 43, 491â498 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2710">64</a></sup> IndelRealignerTargetCreator/IndelRealigner tool (GATK v. 3.7-0) to realign reads in indel regions. Sample alignments were validated at each step using ValidateSam (Picard-tools v. 2.8.2) to ensure no errors in production. Processed alignments were summarized with CollectAlignmentSummaryMetrics (Picard-tools v. 2.8.2) and Samtools [<a href="https://github.com/samtools/samtools">https://github.com/samtools/samtools</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">SNP calling and genotyping</h3><p>SNP-calling and genotyping was performed with the GATK (v. 3.7-0) HaplotypeCaller run in GVCF mode followed by joint-genotyping with GenotypeGVCFs [<a href="https://software.broadinstitute.org/gatk/">https://software.broadinstitute.org/gatk/</a>]. Reads were filtered from the HaplotypeCaller step to exclude those with a mapping quality less than 20 and to exclude those marked as polymerase chain reaction (PCR) duplicates or secondary alignments (see above). This approach yielded 32,384,028 SNPs across all samples. SNP filtering was conducted by applying hard filters to the raw variants using GATK v. 4.0.2.1. We filtered the raw call set to exclude SNPs with low (&lt;785) and high depth (&gt;2862) summed across samples. We also excluded multi-allelic SNPs, SNPs within 10âbp of indel polymorphisms, and SNPs meeting the following conditions: QUALâ&lt;â30 and QDâ&lt;â5.0. Genotypes were set as missing if DP was below 5 or above 20, as well as SNPs with a genotype call rateâ&lt;â80%, or a minor allele frequency below 0.01. We estimated a <i>P</i> value for each site from a HardyâWeinberg Equilibrium test using VCFtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Danecek, P. et al. The variant call format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics 27, 2156â2158 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2739">65</a></sup> and filtered out SNPs showing an excess in heterozygosity (exact test, <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.05). This procedure yielded a filtered call set of 7,149,205 SNPs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Statistical analysis</h3><p>All statistical analysis was conducted in the R statistical computing language unless otherwise indicated.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">LD analysis</h3><p>LD was estimated using a method for estimating <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> that is appropriate for unphased data (see VCFtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Danecek, P. et al. The variant call format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics 27, 2156â2158 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2766">65</a></sup>). The LD decay curve for the GWAS panel was calculated as in Flowers et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Flowers, J. M. et al. Cross-species hybridization and the origin of North African date palms. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 1â8. &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1817453116&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2770">4</a></sup>. Briefly, <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> was calculated for unphased SNPs with minor allele frequency greater than 10% using theâgeno-ld option in VCFtools (v. 0.1.14). Decay curves were generated by fitting a curve to the pairwise <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> estimates by physical distance between SNP pairs with nonlinear least squares using an approach adapted from Marroni et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Marroni, F. et al. Nucleotide diversity and linkage disequilibrium in Populus nigra cinnamyl alcohol dehydrogenase (CAD4) gene. Tree Genet. Genomes 7, 1011â1023 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2783">66</a></sup>. The half-decay distance was then calculated as the distance at which <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> is half its maximum value (i.e., 1âbp distance).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Characterization of fruit color</h3><p>Eight khalal stage fruits free of injury per date palm variety were harvested, rinsed with tap water to remove any dust and then air-dried. The fruits were sliced longitudinally, and fruit color was then measured using two strategies. First, we photographed the sliced fruits with a color checker in a camera photo studio box, where the pictures were taken on a white background with a digital camera. The color of the fruit was analyzed with ImageJ software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Abramoff, M. D. et al. Image processing with imageJ. Biophotonics Int. 11, 36â41 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2799">67</a></sup> using the RGB color parameters.</p><p>Second, we used a complementary approach, where we used Tomato Analyzer software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Darrigues, A. et al. Tomato Analyzer-color Test: a new tool for efficient digital phenotyping. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 133, 579â586 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2806">68</a></sup> v.2.2 to obtain estimates of color parameters L*, a*, b*. The L* coordinate expresses the darkness and the lightness of the color and ranges from black (0) to white (100). Coordinates a* and b* express color direction, where +a* is in the red direction, âa* in the green direction, +b* in the yellow direction and âb* in the blue direction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Darrigues, A. et al. Tomato Analyzer-color Test: a new tool for efficient digital phenotyping. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 133, 579â586 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2810">68</a></sup>. Image acquisition and analysis was done as described in RodrÃ­guez et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="RodrÃ­guez, G. R. et al. Tomato Analyzer: a useful software application to collect accurate and detailed morphological and colorimetric data from two-dimensional objects. J. Vis. Exp. 37, 1956 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2814">27</a></sup>. Sliced fruits were place on a scanner with a black background and covered to avoid the effects of ambient light. Scanned pictures were saved as JPEG files and the estimates of color parameters L*, a*, b* were done on each fruit. The average of all fruit was calculated. The two methods were highly correlated, so we used color index a*/b* in order to evaluate the differences in skin colors of the fruits and used that for the association study.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Fruit anthocyanin content</h3><p>Total anthocyanin was extracted from three replicates of khalal stage fruit from each date palm variety using fruits snap-frozen on liquid nitrogen following the procedure described in Rabino and Mancinelli<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Rabino, I. &amp; Mancinelli, A. L. Light, temperature, and anthocyanin production. Plant Physiol. 81, 922â924 (1986)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2827">69</a></sup> with minor modification. Briefly, anthocyanin from frozen fruit skin (100âmg) was ground into fine powder and extracted in 1âml of acidic methanol (1% HCl) by incubation at room temperature in the dark for 18âh, followed by centrifugation for 10âmin at 12,000â<i>g</i>. Quantification of total anthocyanin was done using the absorbance measured by a spectrophotometer using the equation</p><p>Total anthocyaninâ=â(A530-0.25âÃâA657)/FW, where A530 and A657 nm are the absorbance and FW is the wet weight of the plant material (g).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Fruit size</h3><p>Fruit photographs used for color analysis (see above) included a ruler as a size standard. ImageJ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Abramoff, M. D. et al. Image processing with imageJ. Biophotonics Int. 11, 36â41 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2845">67</a></sup> (v. 2) and Tomato analyzer software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="RodrÃ­guez, G. R. et al. Tomato Analyzer: a useful software application to collect accurate and detailed morphological and colorimetric data from two-dimensional objects. J. Vis. Exp. 37, 1956 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2849">27</a></sup> were then used to estimate fruit length and width.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Fruit sugar and acid content</h3><p>Fruit sucrose, glucose, and fructose were quantified from 125 varieties at the tamar stage when fruits are dry, ripening is complete and the stage at which dates are typically consumed. Fruits were snap-frozen at â20âÂ°C and between 10 and 15 fruits per variety were immediately maintained at â20âÂ°C through arrival at Montpellier (French Agricultural Research Centre for International Development, CIRAD) where high performance liquid chromatography analysis was performed. A single measurement from two pooled fruits was obtained for each of the sugar and acid traits. Date pieces (without the stone) were frozen with liquid nitrogen and ground in powder, put in two separate tight glass vials, stored at â20âÂ°C until sampling. For the dry matter, in duplicate, 1âg of sample was weighed and placed in a stove under vacuum at 70âÂ°C for 72âh. A control was checked for 4 days to determine the optimum duration. Sugar extractions were performed using the method adapted from Bchir et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Bchir, B. et al. Effect of air-drying conditions on physico-chemical properties of osmotically pre-treated pomegranate seeds. Food Bioprocess Technol. 5, 1840â1852 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2861">70</a></sup>. For each sample, 500âmg date paste and 10âml of 80% ethanol were placed in a 15âml tube, heated for 5âmin at 80âÂ°C in a water bath. Each tube was then agitated at first manually and then mechanically for 15âmin for better spreading. After centrifugation at 9000âÃâ<i>g</i> (Avanti J-E centrifuge; Beckman-Coulter, Brea, CA, USA), the bottom was extracted twice and the supernatants gathered, filtered at 0.45âÂµm and injected. The method was tested with acidic water (0.01âN H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>). Sample standards were Sigma-Aldrich (St. Louis, MO, USA) were used.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Fruit moisture content</h3><p>Fruit sampling was performed as in fruit sugar and acid content section above. Date pulp from two fruits was recovered and ground with liquid nitrogen to homogenize sample and stored at â80âÂ°C to obtain a single measurement per variety. The moisture content was gravimetrically determined by measuring the weight loss of 2.5âg of date pulp samples, dried at 70âÂ°C until the samples reached a stable weight.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Genome-wide association analysis</h3><p>We ran the genome-wide association mapping analysis using the Gapit R package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Lipka, A. E. et al. GAPIT: Genome association and prediction integrated tool. Bioinformatics 28, 2397â2399 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2888">25</a></sup>. For computational efficiency and to minimize multiple-testing issues but provide dense coverage with respect to the LD decay distance, we used a 5.5% downsampled random SNP set (392,948 SNPs). A CMLM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Zhang, Z. et al. Mixed linear model approach adapted for genome-wide association studies. Nat. Genet. 42, 355â360 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2892">26</a></sup> using both population structure and kinship information as covariates was performed on the genotypes from the 157 date palm samples. Population structure was inferred with a principal component analysis (PCA) generated by Gapit using 1% of the SNPs (randomly sampled). Gapit further used the first five components of the PCA (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig1">1a</a>; Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM6">2</a>). Kinship was inferred using the VanRaden algorithm (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM7">3</a>). Significant SNPs were identified using a conservative Bonferroni threshold of <i>P</i>â&lt;â1.27âÃâ10<sup>â7</sup>. For traits with significant results, we further performed a second GWAS analysis using the full SNP set on particular LGs where significant SNPs were identified.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Characterization of <i>Ibn Majid</i> and the <i>VIR</i> gene</h3><p>We previously identified a <i>copia</i>-like retrotransposon insertion polymorphism in exon 3 of an R2R3-MYB transcription factor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Hazzouri, K. M. et al. Whole genome re-sequencing of date palms yields insights into diversification of a fruit tree crop. Nat. Commun. 6, 8824 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2929">13</a></sup> (NCBI Gene ID: LOC103717680) that is orthologous to the <i>Virescens (VIR)</i> gene in oil palm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Singh, R. et al. The oil palm VIRESCENS gene controls fruit colour and encodes a R2R3-MYB. Nat. Commun. 5, 4106 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2936">28</a></sup>. To characterize this retrotransposon, we PCR-amplified the element long terminal repeats (as well as adjacent <i>VIR</i> gene sequence) in Thory and Empress varieties collected from the USDA farm in Thermal, California and the USDA/UC Riverside farm respectively, using GoTaq PCR Core Systems (Promega, Madison, WI USA) buffer and polymerase.</p><p>The primer pairs 5â²-TGT GTC CGG CAT TGC ACT TCT-3â² (forward) and 5â²-GCT CAA TGT TGA TGT TCT TGT TGG-3â² (reverse) were used for the 5â² LTR, and 5â²-ACTC TGA CTA CCA AGT ACT TGA TG-3â² (forward) and 5â²-CTG CAC TAT TAT CAC AGT AGA TGG-3â² (reverse) for the 3â² LTR. Amplified products were sent for Sanger sequencing at GeneWiz (South Plainfield, New Jersey). Our genome assembly also contains a complete copy of the insertion (~11.7âkb). BLAST was used to align the insertion against itself in order to identify the matching long terminal repeat regions. The program LTRdigest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Steinbiss, S., Willhoeft, U., Gremme, G. &amp; Kurtz, S. Fine-grained annotation and classification of de novo predicted LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, 7002â7013 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2946">71</a></sup> was used to confirm the BLAST results. A BLAST search queried the full <i>Ibn Majid</i> sequence against the date palm genome to determine copy number.</p><p>Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">11</a> provides coordinates of our manual annotation of the <i>VIR</i> gene in the BC4 male assembly. Genotyping of the <i>Ibn Majid</i> insertion in <i>VIR</i> exon 3 in date palm varieties was performed by manual inspection of aligned reads spanning the insertion region in JBrowse<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Buels, R. et al. JBrowse: a dynamic web platform for genome visualization and analysis. Genome Biol. 17, 66 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d10202e2968">72</a></sup>. Since the BC4 male genome assembly has the insertion allele (<i>VIR</i><sup>IM</sup>, see Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig3">3</a>), mapped reads originating from wild type (<i>VIR</i><sup><i>+</i></sup>), or non-insertion alleles, are soft-clipped at the exon 3-insertion boundary. We scored the presence of soft-clipped reads (supporting the presence of a <i>VIR</i><sup>+</sup> allele) or unclipped reads spanning the exon 3-insertion boundary (supporting the presence of a <i>VIR</i><sup>IM</sup> insertion allele) to identify genotypes. We repeated this procedure by examining read alignments at both the 5â² and 3â² ends of the insertion in the BC4 male assembly and samples where both 5â² and 3â² genotypes yielded matching genotypes were retained for analysis. Given our interest in fruit color phenotypes, we genotyped female palms only.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Characterization of invertases and deletion polymorphisms</h3><p>Examination of genes in the sugar composition QTL on LG 14 (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM10">6</a>) initially revealed three positional candidatesâan alkaline/neutral invertase (chr14G0028200) and two adjacent cell wall invertases (chr14G0022900 and chr14G0023100) predicted by our gene annotation pipeline. We checked for potential additional unannotated copies of invertase in this region by aligning predicted transcripts for each of the three genes to this region using the Splign transcript to genomic alignment tool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Kapustin, Y., Souvorov, A., Tatusova, T. &amp; Lipman, D. Splign: algorithms for computing spliced alignments with identification of paralogs. Biol. Direct 3, 20 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d10202e3007">73</a></sup>. This recovered a minus strand sequence (which we refer to as <i>CWINV2</i>), with close homology to the flanking invertases <i>CWINV1</i> and <i>CWINV3</i> at 2,489,373 to 2,485,592, but multiple insertion/deletions in regions homologous to invertase CDS exons.</p><p>Coverage depth for deletion variation analysis was determined in 500âbp non-overlapping bins with samtools bedcov<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d10202e3023">74</a></sup> (v. 1.9) using default settings. Raw depth values were normalized independently for each sample by dividing the raw depth of each bin by the median raw depth of all bins on LG 14 following by log<sub>2</sub> transformation following Flowers et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Flowers, J. M. et al. Whole-genome resequencing reveals extensive natural variation in the model green alga Chlamydomonas reinhardtii. Plant Cell 27, 2353â2369 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d10202e3029">75</a></sup>. Samples were genotyped into homozygous deletion and alternate genotype classes for the 40âkb deletion by manual inspection of Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">12</a>. Homozygous genotypes for the deletion upstream of <i>A/N-INV1</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#Fig4">4</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">13</a>) were called by setting a threshold requiring that at least one 500âbp interval in the 5âkb deletion region have log<sub>2</sub> normalized depth less than â5. At present, it is not possible to distinguish heterozygotes for deletion alleles from insertion homozygotes owing to the moderate coverage in our re-sequencing data.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Invertase enzyme assay</h3><p>Two sucrose and two reducing sugar varieties were chosen for the invertase assay. The experiment was conducted on two days with all four varieties represented by a single fruit on each day. Assays were conducted on one khalal stage fruit snap-frozen at the time of collection (see above) followed by storage at â80âÂ°C. Crude extracts were obtained from the frozen date fruit following the protocol of Hasegawa and Smolensky<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Hasegawa, S. &amp; Smolensky, D. C. Date invertase: properties and activity associated with maturation and quality. J. Agric. Food Chem. 18, 902â904 (1970)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d10202e3056">33</a></sup>. Each frozen fruit was pulverized with mortar and pestle (with seed removed), and then ground in a kitchen blender, and 5âg placed in cold extraction buffer (20âml 4.0% NaCl, 1âg polyvinylpyrrolidone, PVP). An additional maceration step was conducted in a laboratory homogenizer for 1â2âmin. The extract was then centrifuged at 20,000âÃâ<i>g</i> for 15âmin at 4âÂ°C. The supernatant containing soluble invertase was stored on ice and the remainder centrifuged a second time at 20,000âÃâ<i>g</i> for 15âmin at 4âÂ°C. The supernatants were combined and 10âml dialyzed against cold water at 4Â° overnight to remove sugars from the extract. The sample was then split, and one-half of the sample boiled at 100âÂ°C to measure background activity from potential contaminating sugar from the fruit. Invertase activity of unboiled and boiled crude extracts was then measured by colorimetric assay on a Synergy H1 microplate reader with a coupled enzyme assay kit (Sigma catalog no. MAK118) following the manufacturerâs instructions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">Fruit RNA-Seq analysis</h3><p>Two RNA-Seq datasets were collected to address questions about fruit development and variation in fruit traits. RNA-Seq at different fruit development stages was conducted on fruits collected in 2014 from replicate trees located on the grounds of the United Arab Emirates University, Date Palm Tissue Culture Laboratory in Al-Ain, UAE. For this experiment, three or four separate trees of Khenezi (a variety with red fruit) and Khalas (yellow fruit) varieties were sampled repeatedly at 45, 75, 105, 120, and 135 days post pollination and fruits snap-frozen on liquid nitrogen. RNA was extracted from a single fruit from each three or more trees per variety following standard protocols for TruSeq library preparation, and 2âÃâ101âbp paired-end sequencing performed on an Illumina HiSeq 2500.</p><p>A second experiment was conducted on khalal stage fruit collected at the Al-Shuwaib farm in 2016. Three fruits were collected from each of eight palms each of a different variety chosen based on their being at or close to the extremes of the sucrose and reducing sugar type distributions (i.e., high and low sucrose concentration). Fruits were processed as described above and libraries constructed with Nextera library preparation kit (Illumina) and 2âÃâ76âbp paired-end sequencing performed on a NextSeq (Illumina) instrument.</p><p>Differential expression analysis was performed by trimming raw sequencing reads with Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114â2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d10202e3080">45</a></sup> (v 0.36) with parameters ILLUMINACLIP:ãadapter fastaã:2:30:10 TRAILING:3 LEADING:3 SLIDINGWINDOW:4:15 MINLEN:36. Reads were then aligned to the BC4 male reference genome with the STAR split read aligner<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Dobin, A. et al. STAR: ultrafast universal RNA-seq aligner. Bioinformatics 29, 15â21 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d10202e3084">47</a></sup> (v. 2.5.3a) and read counts generated per gene by taking the union of exons with htseq-count<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Anders, S., Pyl, P. T. &amp; Huber, W. HTSeq-A Python framework to work with high-throughput sequencing data. Bioinformatics 31, 166â169 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d10202e3088">76</a></sup> (v. 0.9.1) set to include only uniquely mapped reads (i.e., htseq-count optionsÂ --typeâ=âexon--modeâ=âunion--nonuniqueâ=ânone). Read count normalization was conducted with the median-of-ratios method of DESeq2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Love, M. I., Huber, W. &amp; Anders, S. Moderated estimation of fold change and dispersion for RNA-seq data with DESeq2. Genome Biol. 15, 550 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d10202e3092">77</a></sup> (v. 1.8.2). Tests of differential expression of <i>Virescens</i> (Pdac_HC_chr4G0137100) between red (Khenezi, <i>n</i>â=â3 replicate libraries) and yellow (Khalas, <i>n</i>â=â3 or 4 replicate libraries) varieties were conducted separately for each of the fruit development time points of 45, 75, 105, 120, and 135 days post pollination. <i>P</i> values are reported for a Waldâs test of the hypothesis of no fold-difference between Khenezi and Khalas expression at each stage.</p><p>RNA-seq analysis of differential gene expression of invertases <i>A/N-INV1, CWINV1</i>, and <i>CWINV3</i> (Pdac_HC_chr14G0028200, Pdac_HC_chr14G0022900, and Pdac_HC_chr14G0023100, respectively) between sucrose (<i>n</i>â=â4 varieties) and reducing-sugar types (<i>n</i>â=â4 varieties) was conducted by building three libraries per variety from RNA extracted independently from three different fruits followed by sequencing each library. Analysis of differential expression between sucrose-type and reducing-type varieties was then performed by aligning reads with STAR (see above), counting reads with htseq-count, and generating raw count matrices in DESeq2. Raw counts per gene were then summed across libraries for each variety owing to low read counts in some libraries. Subsequent analysis was conducted by first dropping low count genes (genes with &lt;10 reads summed across all 8 samples) followed by the standard DESeq2 (v. 1.22.2) work flow with four biological replicates (i.e., date palm varieties) in each treatment group. Uncorrected <i>P</i> values for the hypothesis of no differential expression are presented in the main text for three candidate genes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in theÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM3">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>Data supporting the findings of this work are available within the paper and itsÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a> files. A reporting summary for this Article is available as aÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a> file. The datasets generated and analyzed during the current study are available from the corresponding author upon request. The genome and various accessory files have been deposited in the Sequence Read Archive (SRA) under NCBI BioProject PRJNA322046 [<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/322046">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/322046</a>]. Short read sequencing data from the GWAS panel have been deposited in the SRA under <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/PRJNA505141">PRJNA505141</a>. RNA-seq data for differential gene expression experiments have been deposited in the SRA under <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/Traces/study1/?acc=PRJNA505138">PRJNA505138</a>. A genome browser was constructed using Tripal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Ficklin, S. P. et al. Tripal: a construction toolkit for online genome databases. Database 201, bar044 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d10202e3231">78</a></sup> (v2.1) and can be explored visually at [<a href="https://datepalmgenomehub.abudhabi.nyu.edu">https://datepalmgenomehub.abudhabi.nyu.edu</a>]. SNP data for the GWAS have been deposited at the Dryad Digital Repository [<a href="https://doi.org/10.5061/dryad.3mc4265">https://doi.org/10.5061/dryad.3mc4265</a>]. The source data underlying Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM11">1</a>bâg, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM11">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM11">3</a>b, c, eâg, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM11">4</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM11">5</a>, Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM11">1</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM11">13</a>, and Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM11">12</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12604-9#MOESM11">14</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p>
